<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the history of humanity. For the entire history of Earth, see <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">History of Earth</a>. For the field of historical study, see <a href="/wiki/World_history" title="World history">World history</a>. For humanity's evolutionary history, see <a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">Human evolution</a>.</div>

<span style="font-size: 120%;">↓</span> <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">Future</a> <span class="nowrap">  </span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:#f8eaba;padding:0.4em;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_history" title="Template:Human history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_history" title="Template talk:Human history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Human_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>
<b>Human history</b>, also called <b>world history</b>, is the narrative of <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humanity</a>'s past. It is understood and studied through <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">invention of writing</a>, human <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a> has been studied through <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">primary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_source" title="Secondary source">secondary source</a> documents.
</p><p>Humanity's written history was preceded by its <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> ("Old Stone Age") era. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> ("New Stone Age") era, which saw the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Agricultural Revolution</a> begin in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> between 10,000 and 5,000 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a>. During this period, humans began the systematic <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">husbandry</a> of plants and animals. As agriculture advanced, most humans transitioned from a <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> to a settled lifestyle as farmers in <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">permanent settlements</a>. The relative security and increased productivity provided by farming allowed communities to expand into increasingly larger units, fostered by advances in <a href="/wiki/History_of_transport" title="History of transport">transportation</a>.
</p><p>The earliest complex societies appeared in fertile river valleys. As farming developed, <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grain agriculture</a> became more sophisticated and prompted a <a href="/wiki/Division_of_labour" title="Division of labour">division of labour</a> to store food between growing seasons. Labor divisions led to the rise of a leisured <a href="/wiki/Upper_class" title="Upper class">upper class</a> and the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cities" title="Cities">cities</a>, which provided <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">the foundation</a> for <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a>. The growing complexity of human societies necessitated systems of <a href="/wiki/Accounting" title="Accounting">accounting</a> and writing. <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> developed in the late <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> on the Indian subcontinent, while the <a href="/wiki/Axial_Age" title="Axial Age">Axial Age</a> witnessed the introduction of religions such as <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>.
</p><p>With civilizations flourishing, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient history</a> saw the rise and fall of empires. <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">Post-classical history</a> (the "<a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>" from about 500 to 1500 CE) witnessed the rise of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> (from around 1300 CE). The 15th-century introduction of <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type printing</a> in Europe revolutionized communication and facilitated widespread dissemination of information, hastening the end of the Middle Ages and ushering in the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">Scientific Revolution</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a> lasted from about 1500 to 1800 CE. Events during this period included the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>. By the 18th century, the accumulation of knowledge and technology had reached a <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass_(sociodynamics)" title="Critical mass (sociodynamics)">critical mass</a> that brought about the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> and began the <a href="/wiki/Late_modern_period" title="Late modern period">late modern period</a>, which started around 1800 CE and continues.
</p><p>The above scheme of historical <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periodization</a> (dividing history into antiquity, post-classical, early modern, and late modern periods) was developed for, and applies best to, the history of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, particularly Europe and the Mediterranean. Outside this region, including <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">Indian</a> civilizations, historical timelines unfolded differently up to the 18th century. By that century, due to extensive <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a>, the histories of most civilizations had become substantially intertwined. Over the last quarter-millennium, the rates of growth of <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population</a>, agriculture, industry, commerce, scientific knowledge, technology, communications, weapons destructiveness, and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a> have greatly accelerated.
</p>

<h2><span id="Prehistory_.282.5_million_years_ago.E2.80.93600_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_(2.5_million_years_ago–600_BC)">Prehistory (2.5 million years ago–600 BC)</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">Prehistory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_prehistory" title="Timeline of prehistory">Timeline of prehistory</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_evolution">Human evolution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">Human evolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucy_Skeleton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1362" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Lucy_Skeleton.jpg/170px-Lucy_Skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Lucy_Skeleton.jpg/255px-Lucy_Skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Lucy_Skeleton.jpg/340px-Lucy_Skeleton.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucy_Skeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Lucy_(Australopithecus)" title="Lucy (Australopithecus)">Lucy</a><i>,</i> the first <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Australopithecus afarensis</a></i> skeleton found</div></div></div>
<p>Humans evolved in Africa from other primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.20151_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.20151-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">Genetic</a> measurements indicate that the ape lineage which would lead to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee%E2%80%93human_last_common_ancestor" title="Chimpanzee–human last common ancestor">diverged</a> from the lineage that would lead to <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bonobo" title="Bonobo">bonobos</a>, the closest living relatives of modern humans, somewhere between 13 million and 5 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patterson2006_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson2006-5">[5]</a></sup> The term <a href="/wiki/Hominini" title="Hominini">hominin</a> denotes human ancestors that lived after the split with chimpanzees and bonobos,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunbar20168_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunbar20168-6">[6]</a></sup> including many species and at least two distinct <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cela-Conde_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cela-Conde-7">[7]</a></sup> Other fossil specimens such as <i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus" title="Paranthropus">Paranthropus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kenyanthropus" title="Kenyanthropus">Kenyanthropus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Orrorin" title="Orrorin">Orrorin</a></i> may represent additional genera, but palaeontologists debate their taxonomic satus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cela-Conde_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cela-Conde-7">[7]</a></sup> The early hominins such as <i>Australopithecus</i> had the same brain size as apes but were distinguished from apes by <a href="/wiki/Bipedalism" title="Bipedalism">walking on two legs</a>, an adaptation perhaps associated with a shift from forest to savanna habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunbar20168,_10_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunbar20168,_10-8">[8]</a></sup> Hominins began to use rudimentary stone tools <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 3.3 million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[a]</a></sup> marking the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torre2019_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Torre2019-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The genus <i>Homo</i> evolved from <i>Australopithecus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> The earliest members of <i>Homo</i> share key traits with <i>Australopithecus</i>, but tend to have larger brains and smaller teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Villmoare2015_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Villmoare2015-17">[16]</a></sup> The earliest record of <i>Homo</i> is the 2.8 million-year-old specimen <a href="/wiki/LD_350-1" title="LD 350-1">LD 350-1</a> from Ethiopia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Villmoare2015_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Villmoare2015-17">[16]</a></sup> and the earliest named species is <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_habilis" title="Homo habilis">Homo habilis</a></i> which evolved by 2.3 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">H. erectus</a></i> (the African variant is sometimes called <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">H. ergaster</a></i>) evolved by 2 million years ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herries_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herries-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[b]</a></sup> and was the first hominin species to <a href="/wiki/Early_expansions_of_hominins_out_of_Africa" title="Early expansions of hominins out of Africa">leave Africa</a> and disperse across Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunbar201610_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunbar201610-22">[20]</a></sup> Perhaps as early as 1.5 million years ago, but certainly by 250,000 years ago, humans <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">began to use fire</a> for heat and cooking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian201511_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian201511-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning about 500,000 years ago, <i>Homo</i> diversified into many new species of <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">archaic humans</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a> in Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovans</a> in Siberia, and the diminutive <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_floresiensis" title="Homo floresiensis">H. floresiensis</a></i> in Indonesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015400_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015400-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunbar201611_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunbar201611-26">[24]</a></sup> Human evolution was not a simple linear or branched progression but involved <a href="/wiki/Interbreeding_between_archaic_and_modern_humans" title="Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans">interbreeding between related species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> Genomic research has shown that hybridization between substantially diverged lineages was common in human evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ackermann_2015_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ackermann_2015-29">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> evidence suggests that several genes of Neanderthal origin are present among all non-African populations, and Neanderthals and other hominins, such as Denisovans, may have contributed up to 6% of their <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> to present-day humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21179161_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21179161-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1126/science.1209202_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1126/science.1209202-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_humans">Early humans</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_human" title="Early modern human">Early modern human</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_human_migrations" title="Early human migrations">Early human migrations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lascaux2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="598" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Lascaux2.jpg/220px-Lascaux2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Lascaux2.jpg/330px-Lascaux2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Lascaux2.jpg/440px-Lascaux2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lascaux2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cave painting, <a href="/wiki/Lascaux" title="Lascaux">Lascaux, France</a>, c. 15,000 BCE</div></div></div>
<p><i>Homo sapiens</i> emerged in Africa around 300,000 years ago from a species commonly designated as either <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">H. heidelbergensis</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_rhodesiensis" title="Homo rhodesiensis">H. rhodesiensis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> Humans in this era were <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_human" title="Early modern human">anatomically modern</a>, but full <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioural_modernity" title="Behavioural modernity">behavioural modernity</a> did not appear until between 70,000 and 50,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015343–4_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015343–4-33">[31]</a></sup> By that point humans exhibited many characteristic behaviours such as burial of the dead, use of jewellery and projectile weapons, and seafaring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015319–20,_330,_354_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015319–20,_330,_354-34">[32]</a></sup> One of the most important changes (the date of which is unknown) was the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_language" title="Origin of language">development of syntactic language</a>, which dramatically improved humans' ability to communicate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015344–6_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015344–6-35">[33]</a></sup> Signs of early artistic expression can be found in the form of <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">cave paintings</a> and sculptures made from ivory, stone, and bone, implying a form of spirituality generally interpreted as <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNeillMcNeill200317–18_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNeillMcNeill200317–18-36">[34]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015357–8,_409_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015357–8,_409-37">[35]</a></sup> Paleolithic humans lived as <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> and were generally <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian201522_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian201522-38">[36]</a></sup> They inhabited grasslands or sparsely wooded areas and avoided dense forest cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gavashelishvili23_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gavashelishvili23-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_migrations_mercator.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="1173" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/350px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/525px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/700px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_migrations_mercator.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview map of the peopling of the world by early humans during the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, following the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Dispersal" title="Southern Dispersal">Southern Dispersal</a> paradigm. Labels are in thousands of years ago.</div></div></div>
<p>The migration of anatomically modern humans <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">out of Africa</a> took place in multiple waves beginning 194,000–177,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[c]</a></sup> The dominant view among scholars (<a href="/wiki/Southern_Dispersal" title="Southern Dispersal">Southern Dispersal</a>) is that the early waves of migration died out and all modern non-Africans are descended from a single group that left Africa 70,000–50,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015283_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015283-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Posth_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Posth-47">[44]</a></sup> <i>H. sapiens</i> proceeded to colonize all the continents and larger islands, arriving in <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Australia" title="Prehistory of Australia">Australia</a> 65,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> Europe 45,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015283_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015283-45">[42]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">the Americas</a> by 21,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> These migrations occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Period" title="Last Glacial Period">most recent Ice Age</a>, when temperate regions of today were extremely inhospitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015316_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015316-50">[47]</a></sup> Nevertheless, by the end of the Ice Age some 12,000 years ago, humans had colonized nearly all ice-free parts of the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201224–29_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201224–29-51">[48]</a></sup> Human expansion coincided with both the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal_extinction" title="Neanderthal extinction">extinction of the Neanderthals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015321,_406,_440–441_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015321,_406,_440–441-52">[49]</a></sup> These extinctions were probably caused by climate change, human activity, or a combination of the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian2015406_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2015406-54">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_agriculture">Rise of agriculture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a></div>
<p>Beginning around 10,000 BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> marked the development of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, which fundamentally changed the human lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewin_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewin-55">[52]</a></sup> Agriculture began independently in different parts of the globe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephens_897–902_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens_897–902-56">[53]</a></sup> and included a diverse range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxa" title="Taxa">taxa</a>, in at least 11 separate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centers_of_origin" title="Centers of origin">centers of origin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2014_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2014-57">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">Cereal crop</a> cultivation and <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">animal domestication</a> had occurred in the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Middle East</a> by at least 8500 BCE in the form of <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199911_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199911-58">[55]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a> valley in China domesticated <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> around 8000 BCE; the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> valley may have cultivated <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> by 7000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015325,_336_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015325,_336-59">[56]</a></sup> Pigs were the most important domesticated animal in early China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015323_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015323-60">[57]</a></sup> People in Africa's <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> cultivated <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a> and several other crops between 8000 and 5000 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[d]</a></sup> while other agricultural centers arose in the Ethiopian highlands and the West African rain forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201521_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201521-63">[59]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus Valley</a>, crops were cultivated by 7000 BCE and <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> were domesticated by 6500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015265_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015265-64">[60]</a></sup> In the Americas, <a href="/wiki/Cucurbita" title="Cucurbita">squash</a> was cultivated by at least 8500 BCE in South America, and domesticated <a href="/wiki/Arrowroot" title="Arrowroot">arrowroot</a> appeared in Central America by 7800 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015518_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015518-65">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">Potatoes</a> were first cultivated in the Andes Mountains of South America, where the <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> was also domesticated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201236–37_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201236–37-66">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G%C3%B6beklitepe_Building_C_sept_2019_5373crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/G%C3%B6beklitepe_Building_C_sept_2019_5373crop.jpg/150px-G%C3%B6beklitepe_Building_C_sept_2019_5373crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/G%C3%B6beklitepe_Building_C_sept_2019_5373crop.jpg/225px-G%C3%B6beklitepe_Building_C_sept_2019_5373crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/G%C3%B6beklitepe_Building_C_sept_2019_5373crop.jpg/300px-G%C3%B6beklitepe_Building_C_sept_2019_5373crop.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:G%C3%B6beklitepe_Building_C_sept_2019_5373crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pillar from <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a></div></div></div>
<p>There is no scholarly consensus on why the Neolithic Revolution occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015218_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015218-67">[63]</a></sup> For example, in some theories agriculture was the result of an increase in population which led people to seek out new food sources, while in others agriculture was the cause of population growth as the food supply improved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher201595_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher201595-68">[64]</a></sup> Other proposed factors include climate change, resource scarcity, and ideology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015216–218_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015216–218-69">[65]</a></sup> The effects of the transition to agriculture are better understood: it created food surpluses that could support people not directly engaged in food production,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201334–35_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201334–35-70">[66]</a></sup> permitting far denser populations and the creation of the first <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">cities</a> and <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">states</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewin_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewin-55">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cities were centres of <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Power_(social_and_political)" title="Power (social and political)">political power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015313,_391_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015313,_391-71">[67]</a></sup> Cities established a <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiosis</a> with their surrounding <a href="/wiki/Rural_area" title="Rural area">countrysides</a>, absorbing agricultural products and providing, in return, manufactured goods and varying degrees of political control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015193_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015193-72">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015313–316_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015313–316-73">[69]</a></sup> Early <a href="/wiki/Proto-city" title="Proto-city">proto-cities</a> appeared at <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Çatalhöyük">Çatalhöyük</a> around 6000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199913_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199913-74">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_society" title="Pastoral society">Pastoral societies</a> based on nomadic animal herding also developed, mostly in dry areas unsuited for plant cultivation such as the Central Asian <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> or the African <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher161–2,_172–3_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher161–2,_172–3-75">[71]</a></sup> Conflict between nomadic herders and sedentary agriculturalists occurred frequently and became a recurring theme in world history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201599_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201599-76">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">Metalworking</a> was first used in the creation of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> tools and ornaments around 6400 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201521_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201521-63">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">Gold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> soon followed, primarily for use in ornaments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201521_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201521-63">[59]</a></sup> The need for metal ores stimulated trade, as many areas of early human settlement lacked the necessary ores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015574_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher2015574-77">[73]</a></sup> The first signs of <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, an alloy of copper and <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, date to around 2500 BCE, but the alloy did not become widely used until much later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201242–43_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201242–43-78">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Neolithic societies usually worshiped ancestors, sacred places, or <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic</a> <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201519_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201519-79">[75]</a></sup> Entities such as the Sun, Moon, Earth, sky, and sea were often deified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMercer1949259_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMercer1949259-80">[76]</a></sup> The vast complex of <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a> in Turkey, dated 9500–8000 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup> is a spectacular example of a Neolithic religious or civic site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher224_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher224-82">[78]</a></sup> It may have been built by hunter-gatherers rather than a sedentary population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher224_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher224-82">[78]</a></sup> Elaborate mortuary practices developed in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic_B" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic B">Pre-Pottery Neolithic B</a>, in which certain high-status individuals were buried under the floors of houses and the graves were later re-opened for the skulls to be removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher135,_253–5_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher135,_253–5-83">[79]</a></sup> Some of the skulls were then <a href="/wiki/Plastered_human_skulls" title="Plastered human skulls">covered in plaster</a>, painted, and displayed in public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher135_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarkerGoucher135-84">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cradles_of_civilization">Cradles of civilization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">Cradle of civilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2906" data-file-width="4372" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg/220px-All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg/330px-All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg/440px-All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Giza_pyramid_complex" title="Giza pyramid complex">Great Pyramids of Giza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> saw the development of cities, which was synonymous with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a>. Early civilizations arose in close proximity to rivers, first in Lower <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (3000 BCE) with the <a href="/wiki/Tigris%E2%80%93Euphrates_river_system" title="Tigris–Euphrates river system">Tigris and Euphrates</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsLanger200121_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsLanger200121-86">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201254–55_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201254–55-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201353_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201353-88">[84]</a></sup> followed by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian civilization</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile River</a> (3000 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBard200063_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBard200063-89">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201370_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201370-90">[86]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley Civilisation</a> in Pakistan (2500 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti200410–13_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti200410–13-91">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllchinAllchin1997153–168_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllchinAllchin1997153–168-92">[88]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">Chinese civilization</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow Rivers</a> (2200 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200215–42_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200215–42-93">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeeple200614–20_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeeple200614–20-94">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>These societies developed a number of unifying characteristics, including a central government, a complex economy and social structure, sophisticated language and writing systems, and distinct cultures and religions. These cultures variously invented the <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201244_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201244-95">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201359_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201359-96">[92]</a></sup> bronze-working, <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">sailing</a> boats, the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">potter's wheel</a>, woven cloth, construction of monumental buildings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199916_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199916-97">[93]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199918_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199918-98">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shrine" title="Shrine">Shrines</a> developed, which evolved into <a href="/wiki/Temple" title="Temple">temple</a> establishments, complete with a complex hierarchy of <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests and priestesses</a>.
</p><p>Writing facilitated the administration of cities, the expression of ideas, and the preservation of information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201343–46_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201343–46-99">[95]</a></sup> Scholars recognize that writing may have independently developed in at least four ancient civilizations: Mesopotamia (3300 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015118_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015118-100">[96]</a></sup> Egypt (around 3250 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-101">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup> China (1200 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup> and lowland <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a> (by 650 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaganBeck1996_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FaganBeck1996-104">[100]</a></sup> Among the earliest surviving written religious scriptures are the Egyptian <i><a href="/wiki/Pyramid_Texts" title="Pyramid Texts">Pyramid Texts</a></i>, the oldest of which date to between 2400 and 2300 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen20071_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen20071-105">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sumerian_26th_c_Adab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Sumerian_26th_c_Adab.jpg/220px-Sumerian_26th_c_Adab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Sumerian_26th_c_Adab.jpg/330px-Sumerian_26th_c_Adab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Sumerian_26th_c_Adab.jpg/440px-Sumerian_26th_c_Adab.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sumerian_26th_c_Adab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monumental <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">Cuneiform</a> <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">inscription</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, 26th century BCE</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, is the first known complex civilization, having developed the first <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> in the 4th millennium BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201254–55_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201254–55-87">[83]</a></sup> It was these cities that produced the earliest known form of writing, <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform script</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201353–54_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201353–54-106">[102]</a></sup> Cuneiform writing began as a system of <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictographs</a>, whose pictorial representations eventually became simplified and more abstract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201353–54_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad201353–54-106">[102]</a></sup> Cuneiform texts were written by using a blunt <a href="/wiki/Phragmites" title="Phragmites">reed</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Stylus" title="Stylus">stylus</a> to draw <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbols</a> upon <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">clay tablets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201262–63_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201262–63-107">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Transport was facilitated by waterways—by rivers and seas. The <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, at the juncture of three continents, fostered the projection of military power and the exchange of goods, ideas, and inventions. This era also saw new land technologies, such as horse-based cavalry and chariots, that allowed armies to move faster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201535_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201535-108">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>These developments led to the rise of territorial states and <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empires</a>. In Mesopotamia there prevailed a pattern of independent warring city-states and of a loose hegemony shifting from one city to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015257_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015257-109">[105]</a></sup> In Egypt, by contrast, the initial division into <a href="/wiki/Upper_and_Lower_Egypt" title="Upper and Lower Egypt">Upper and Lower Egypt</a> was followed by unification of all the valley around 3100 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBard200057–64_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBard200057–64-110">[106]</a></sup> In Crete the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> had entered the Bronze Age by 2700 BCE and is regarded as the first civilization in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201276–77_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis201276–77-111">[107]</a></sup> Around 2600 BCE the Indus Valley Civilization built major cities at <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a> and developed <a href="/wiki/Indus_script" title="Indus script">a writing system</a> of over 400 symbols, which remains undeciphered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015320_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015320-112">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201546_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201546-113">[109]</a></sup> China entered the Bronze Age by 2000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201580_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201580-114">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> (1766–1045 BCE) was the first to use writing, inscribing the results of <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> ceremonies on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oracle_bones" title="Oracle bones">oracle bones</a> – ox shoulder blades and turtle shells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201580_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201580-114">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015136_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoffee2015136-115">[111]</a></sup>
In the 25th–21st centuries BCE, the empires of <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkad</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Sumerian_Empire" title="Neo-Sumerian Empire">Sumer</a> arose in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199936–37_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNeill199936–37-116">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over the following millennia, civilizations developed across the world. By 1600 BCE, <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a> began to develop,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriceThonemann201022_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriceThonemann201022-117">[113]</a></sup> and ended with the <a href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Late Bronze Age collapse">Late Bronze Age collapse</a> that started to affect many Mediterranean civilizations between 1200 and 1150 BCE. In India, this era was the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a> (1750–600 BCE), which laid the foundations of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and other cultural aspects of early Indian society, and ended in the 6th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad2013116–122_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad2013116–122-118">[114]</a></sup> From around 550 BCE, many independent kingdoms and republics known as the <a href="/wiki/Mahajanapadas" title="Mahajanapadas">Mahajanapadas</a> were established across the subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2008260–264_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2008260–264-119">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">Trade</a> increasingly became a source of power as states with access to important resources or controlling important trade routes rose to dominance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>As complex civilizations arose in the Eastern Hemisphere, the indigenous societies in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> remained relatively simple and fragmented into diverse regional cultures. They had developed agriculture by about 5000 BCE, growing <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, beans, <a href="/wiki/Cucurbita" title="Cucurbita">squash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capsicum" title="Capsicum">peppers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomatoes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201516_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.201516-121">[117]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Formative_stage" title="Formative stage">formative stage</a> in Mesoamerica (about 1200 BCE to 250 CE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholsPool2012118,_121_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholsPool2012118,_121-122">[118]</a></sup> more complex and centralized civilizations began to develop, mostly in what is now Mexico, Central America, and Peru. They included civilizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Olmecs" title="Olmecs">Olmecs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholsPool2012118_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholsPool2012118-123">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholsPool2012120_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholsPool2012120-124">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavín</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015199_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015199-125">[121]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Moche_culture" title="Moche culture">Moche</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015216_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015216-126">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Classical_antiquity_.28600_BC.E2.80.93AD_476.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity_(600_BC–AD_476)">Classical antiquity (600 BC–AD 476)</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">Ancient history</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_ancient_history" title="Timeline of ancient history">Timeline of ancient history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Axial_Age">Axial Age</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Axial_Age" title="Axial Age">Axial Age</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_philosophy" title="History of philosophy">History of philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_religion" title="Timeline of religion">Timeline of religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">History of religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2894" data-file-width="1746" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/170px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/255px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/340px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a></div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in the 8th century BCE, the "Axial Age" saw the development of a set of transformative philosophical and religious ideas, mostly independently, in many different places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup> Chinese <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNeillMcNeill200367_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNeillMcNeill200367-128">[124]</a></sup> Indian <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a> <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> are all claimed by some scholars to have developed in the 6th century BCE. (<a href="/wiki/Karl_Jaspers" title="Karl Jaspers">Karl Jaspers</a>' Axial-Age theory also includes <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persian</a> <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, but other scholars dispute his timeline for Zoroastrianism.) In the 5th century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> made substantial advances in the development of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNeillMcNeill200373–74_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNeillMcNeill200373–74-129">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the East, three schools of thought would dominate Chinese thinking well into the 20th century. These were <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>. The Confucian tradition, which would become particularly dominant, looked for <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> not to the force of law but to the power and example of <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a>. Confucianism would later spread to <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015314_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015314-130">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the West, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> philosophical tradition, represented by <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, and other philosophers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsLanger200163_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsLanger200163-131">[127]</a></sup> along with accumulated science, technology, and culture, diffused throughout <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, Egypt, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, and Northwest India, starting in the 4th century BCE after the conquests of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonians" title="Ancient Macedonians">Macedon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsLanger200170–71_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsLanger200170–71-132">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_empires">Regional empires</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">Civilization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">Empire</a></div>
<p>The millennium from 500 BCE to 500 CE saw a series of empires of unprecedented size develop. Well-trained professional armies, unifying ideologies, and advanced bureaucracies created the possibility for emperors to rule over large domains whose populations could attain numbers upwards of tens of millions of subjects.
The great <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empires</a> depended on <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> <a href="/wiki/Annexation" title="Annexation">annexation</a> of territory and on the formation of defended settlements to become agricultural centres. The relative peace that the empires brought encouraged <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>, most notably the massive trade routes in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a>, the maritime trade web in the Indian Ocean,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015233_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015233-133">[129]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015229_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015229-134">[130]</a></sup> In southern Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> (and later the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>), in an era known as "<a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>," established cultures whose practices, laws, and customs are considered the foundation of contemporary <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1284" data-file-width="1908" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg/220px-Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg/330px-Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg/440px-Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, 6th century BCE</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ashoka_pillar_at_Vaishali,_Bihar,_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ashoka_pillar_at_Vaishali%2C_Bihar%2C_India.jpg/220px-Ashoka_pillar_at_Vaishali%2C_Bihar%2C_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ashoka_pillar_at_Vaishali%2C_Bihar%2C_India.jpg/330px-Ashoka_pillar_at_Vaishali%2C_Bihar%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ashoka_pillar_at_Vaishali%2C_Bihar%2C_India.jpg/440px-Ashoka_pillar_at_Vaishali%2C_Bihar%2C_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ashoka_pillar_at_Vaishali,_Bihar,_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Ashoka" title="Pillars of Ashoka">Pillar</a> erected by India's Maurya Emperor <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stela_aksum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="511" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stela_aksum.jpg/220px-Stela_aksum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stela_aksum.jpg/330px-Stela_aksum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stela_aksum.jpg/440px-Stela_aksum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stela_aksum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Obelisk_of_Axum" title="Obelisk of Axum">Obelisk of Axum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a></div></div></div>
<p>There were a number of regional empires during this period. The kingdom of the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> helped to destroy the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian Empire</a> in tandem with the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>, the capital of Assyria, was sacked by the Medes in 612 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad2013110_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertsWestad2013110-135">[131]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Median Empire</a> gave way to successive <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Iranian</a> empires, including the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> (550–330 BCE), the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> (247 BCE–224 CE), and the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> (224–651 CE).
</p><p>Several empires began in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a>, founded in 477 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin2000106–107_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin2000106–107-136">[132]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League#The_Athenian_Empire_(454–404_BC)" title="Delian League">Athenian Empire</a> (454–404 BCE) were two such examples. Later, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> (356–323 BCE), of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedon</a>, founded an empire extending from present-day Greece to present-day India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGolden201125_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGolden201125-137">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[134]</a></sup> The empire divided shortly after his death, but resulted in the spread of Greek culture throughout conquered regions, a process referred to as <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> lasted from 323 to 31 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Asia, the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a> (322–185 BCE) existed in present-day <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">India</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[136]</a></sup> in the 3rd century BCE, most of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> was united to the Maurya Empire by <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a> and flourished under <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka the Great</a>. From the 3rd century CE, the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta dynasty</a> oversaw the period referred to as ancient India's Golden Age. From the 4th to 6th centuries, northern India was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta Empire</a>. In southern India, three prominent <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_peoples" title="Dravidian peoples">Dravidian</a> kingdoms emerged: the <a href="/wiki/Chera_dynasty" title="Chera dynasty">Cheras</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chola_dynasty" title="Chola dynasty">Cholas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[138]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pandya_dynasty" title="Pandya dynasty">Pandyas</a>. The ensuing stability contributed to heralding in the golden age of <a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindu</a> culture in the 4th and 5th centuries.
</p><p>In Europe, the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="History of the Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, centered in present-day <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, began in the 7th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis2012106–107_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart-Davis2012106–107-143">[139]</a></sup> In the 3rd century BCE the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> began expanding its territory through conquest and alliances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly20074–6_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly20074–6-144">[140]</a></sup> By the time of <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> (63 BCE–14 CE), the first Roman Emperor, Rome had already established dominion over most of the Mediterranean. The empire would continue to grow, controlling much of the land from <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, reaching its greatest extent under the emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> (died 117 CE). In the 3rd century CE, the empire split into western and eastern regions, with (usually) separate emperors. The Western empire would <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall</a>, in 476 CE, to German influence under <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a>. The eastern empire, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, with its capital at <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, would continue for another thousand years, until <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Constantinople was conquered</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1453. During most of its existence, the Byzantine Empire was one of the most powerful economic, cultural, and military forces in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup> And <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> is generally considered to be the center of "<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox civilization</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[143]</a></sup> Although the Roman state continued and its traditions were maintained, modern historians distinguish Byzantium from <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">its earlier incarnation</a> because it was centred on Constantinople, oriented towards Greek rather than Latin culture, and characterized by <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Christianity</a>.
</p><p>In China, the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (221–206 BCE), the first imperial dynasty of China, was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han Empire</a> (202 BCE–220 CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015160_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015160-148">[144]</a></sup> The Han dynasty was comparable in power and influence to the Roman Empire that lay at the other end of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015143_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015143-149">[145]</a></sup> Han China developed advanced cartography, shipbuilding, and navigation. The Chinese invented <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a>, and created finely tuned copper instruments. As with other empires during the Classical Period, Han China advanced significantly in the areas of government, education, mathematics, astronomy, technology, and many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1_Mayan_observatory.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="853" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1_Mayan_observatory.jpg/220px-Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1_Mayan_observatory.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1_Mayan_observatory.jpg/330px-Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1_Mayan_observatory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1_Mayan_observatory.jpg/440px-Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1_Mayan_observatory.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1_Mayan_observatory.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a> <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a></div></div></div>
<p>In Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Kingdom of Aksum</a>, centred in present-day Ethiopia, established itself by the 1st century CE as a major trading empire, dominating its neighbours in South <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kush</a> and controlling the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> trade. It minted its own currency and carved enormous monolithic <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stelae</a> to mark its emperors' graves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIliffe200741_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIliffe200741-151">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Successful regional empires were also established in the Americas, arising from cultures established as early as 2500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFagan2005390,_396_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFagan2005390,_396-152">[148]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Mesoamerica</a>, vast pre-Columbian societies were built, the most notable being the <a href="/wiki/Zapotec_civilization" title="Zapotec civilization">Zapotec Empire</a> (700 BCE–1521 CE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[149]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization</a>, which reached its highest state of development during the Mesoamerican Classic period (c. 250–900 CE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoe201191_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoe201191-154">[150]</a></sup> but continued throughout the Post-Classic period until the arrival of the Spanish in the 16th century CE. Maya civilization arose as the <a href="/wiki/Olmecs" title="Olmecs">Olmec</a> <a href="/wiki/Mother_culture" title="Mother culture">mother culture</a> gradually declined. The great Mayan <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> slowly rose in number and prominence, and Maya culture spread throughout the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán</a> and surrounding areas. The later empire of the <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztecs</a> was built on neighbouring cultures and was influenced by conquered peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Toltec" title="Toltec">Toltecs</a>.
</p><p>Some areas experienced slow but steady technological advances, with important developments such as the <a href="/wiki/Stirrup" title="Stirrup">stirrup</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moldboard_plough" title="Moldboard plough">moldboard plough</a> arriving every few centuries. There were, however, in some regions, periods of rapid technological progress. Most important, perhaps, was the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> in the region of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a>, during which hundreds of technologies were invented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[151]</a></sup> Such periods were followed by periods of technological decay, as during the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>'s decline and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall</a> and the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">early medieval</a> period.
</p>
<h3><span id="Declines.2C_falls.2C_and_resurgence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Declines,_falls,_and_resurgence">Declines, falls, and resurgence</span></h3>
<p>The ancient empires faced common problems associated with maintaining huge armies and supporting a central bureaucracy. These costs fell most heavily on the <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasantry</a>, while land-owning <a href="/wiki/Magnate" title="Magnate">magnates</a> increasingly evaded centralized control and its costs. <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">Barbarian</a> pressure on the frontiers hastened internal dissolution. <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> fell into civil war in 220 CE, beginning the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period, while its <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> counterpart became increasingly decentralized and divided about the same time in what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015158,_170_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulliet_et_al.2015158,_170-156">[152]</a></sup> The great empires of Eurasia were all located on temperate and subtropical coastal plains. From the <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> steppes, horse-based nomads, mainly Mongols and Turks, dominated a large part of the continent. The development of the stirrup and the breeding of horses strong enough to carry a fully armed archer made the nomads a constant threat to the more settled civilizations.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pantheon_Rome_04_2016_6460.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Pantheon_Rome_04_2016_6460.jpg/220px-Pantheon_Rome_04_2016_6460.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Pantheon_Rome_04_2016_6460.jpg/330px-Pantheon_Rome_04_2016_6460.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Pantheon_Rome_04_2016_6460.jpg/440px-Pantheon_Rome_04_2016_6460.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pantheon_Rome_04_2016_6460.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a> in Rome, Italy, originally a Roman temple, now a Catholic church</div></div></div>
<p>The gradual break-up of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, spanning several centuries after the 2nd century CE, coincided with the spread of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> outward from the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsLanger200195,_99_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsLanger200195,_99-157">[153]</a></sup> The Western Roman Empire fell under the domination of <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_Germanic_peoples" title="List of ancient Germanic peoples">Germanic tribes</a> in the 5th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins199980–99_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins199980–99-158">[154]</a></sup> and these <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polities</a> gradually developed into a number of warring states, all associated in one way or another with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins1999100–115_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins1999100–115-159">[155]</a></sup> The remaining part of the Roman
